active environment, and the visibility that Cloudflare Radar provides on this dynamic landscape has evolved and expanded over time. To that end, during 2023âs Security Week, we &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/radar-url-scanner-early-access/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;launched our URL Scanner&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, which enables users to safely scan any URL to determine if it is safe to view or interact with. During 2024âs Security Week, we &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/email-security-insights-on-cloudflare-radar/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;launched an Email Security page&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, which provides a unique perspective on the threats posed by malicious emails, spam volume, the adoption of &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/learning/email-security/dmarc-dkim-spf/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;email authentication methods like SPF, DMARC, and DKIM&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, and the use of IPv4/IPv6 and TLS by email servers. For Security Week 2025, we are adding several new DDoS-focused graphs, new insights into leaked credential trends, and a new &lt;b&gt;Bots&lt;/b&gt; page to Cloudflare Radar.Â  We are also taking this opportunity to refactor Radarâs &lt;b&gt;Security &amp;amp; Attacks&lt;/b&gt; page, breaking it out into &lt;b&gt;Application Layer&lt;/b&gt; and &lt;b&gt;Network Layer&lt;/b&gt; sections.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Below, we review all of these changes and additions to Radar.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;layered-security\&quot;&gt;Layered security&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#layered-security\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;Since Cloudflare Radar launched in 2020, it has included both network layer (Layers 3 &amp;amp; 4) and application layer (Layer 7) attack traffic insights on a single &lt;b&gt;Security &amp;amp; Attacks&lt;/b&gt; page. Over the last four-plus years, we have evolved some of the existing data sets on the page, as well as adding new ones. As the page has grown and improved over time, it risked becoming unwieldy to navigate, making it hard to find the graphs and data of interest. To help address that, the &lt;b&gt;Security&lt;/b&gt; section on Radar now features separate &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer\&quot;&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;u&gt;Application Layer&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/network-layer\&quot;&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;u&gt;Network Layer&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/a&gt; pages. The &lt;b&gt;Application Layer&lt;/b&gt; page is the default, and includes insights from analysis of HTTP-based malicious and attack traffic. The &lt;b&gt;Network Layer&lt;/b&gt; page includes insights from analysis of network and transport layer attacks, as well as observed &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/tcp-resets-timeouts/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;TCP resets and timeouts&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. Future security and attack-related data sets will be added to the relevant page. &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/email-security\&quot;&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;u&gt;Email Security&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/a&gt; remains on its own dedicated page.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/3zxFA8iG7N9MZQzAQAaCNf/ee92891b74b0d70052cc43239dad656f/image__2_.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;2400\&quot; height=\&quot;704\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;a-geographic-and-network-view-of-application-layer-ddos-attacks\&quot;&gt;A geographic and network view of application layer DDoS attacks&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#a-geographic-and-network-view-of-application-layer-ddos-attacks\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; have historically provided insights, aggregated on a quarterly basis, into the top source and target locations of application layer DDoS attacks. A &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer#application-layer-ddos-attacks-distribution\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;new map and table&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; on Radarâs &lt;b&gt;Application Layer&lt;/b&gt; Security page now provide more timely insights, with a global choropleth map showing a geographical distribution of source and target locations, and an accompanying list of the top 20 locations by share of all DDoS requests. Source location attribution continues to rely on the geolocation of the IP address originating the blocked request, while target location remains the billing location of the account that owns the site being attacked.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Over the first week of March 2025, the United States, Indonesia, and Germany were the top sources of application layer DDoS attacks, together accounting for over 30% of such attacks as shown below. The concentration across the top targeted locations was quite different, with customers from Canada, the United States, and Singapore attracting 56% of application layer DDoS attacks.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/7nW8CyC5L4s68ntQEe1pfT/a8b571b9b2325f5f71936367e8879af1/image10.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1834\&quot; height=\&quot;1216\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;In addition to extended visibility into the geographic source of application layer DDoS attacks, we have also added &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/learning/network-layer/what-is-an-autonomous-system/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;autonomous system (AS)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;-level visibility. A &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer#application-layer-ddos-attacks-source-as-distribution\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;new treemap&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; view shows the distribution of these attacks by source AS. At a global level, the largest sources include cloud/hosting providers in Germany, the United States, China, and Vietnam.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/332A6OGGKUNCE0wLVBKBmd/2b93c6bd46105602214d7ced2ead71b6/image7.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1834\&quot; height=\&quot;1020\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;For a selected country/region, the treemap displays a source AS distribution for attacks observed to be originating from that location. In some, the sources of attack traffic are heavily concentrated in consumer/business network providers, such as in &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer/pt#application-layer-ddos-attacks-source-as-distribution\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Portugal&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, shown below. However, in other countries/regions that have a large cloud provider presence, such as &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer/ie#application-layer-ddos-attacks-source-as-distribution\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Ireland&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer/sg#application-layer-ddos-attacks-source-as-distribution\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Singapore&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, and the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer/us#application-layer-ddos-attacks-source-as-distribution\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;United States&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, ASNs associated with these types of providers are the dominant sources. To that end, Singapore was listed as being among the top sources of application layer DDoS attacks in each of the quarterly DDoS threat reports in 2024.Â &lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/3RpwYjFjmTR2WFx5UpsUuS/5211c6e5afd4733185ba1ed03750d1d2/image3.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1834\&quot; height=\&quot;1020\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;have-you-been-pwned\&quot;&gt;Have you been pwned?&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#have-you-been-pwned\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;Every week, it seems like thereâs another headline about a data breach, talking about thousands or millions of usernames and passwords being stolen. Or maybe you get an email from an identity monitoring service that your username and password were found on the âdark webâ. (Of course, youâre getting those alerts thanks to a complementary subscription to the service offered as penance from another data breachâ¦)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This credential theft is especially problematic because people often reuse passwords, despite best practices advising the use of strong, unique passwords for each site or application. To help mitigate this risk, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/a-safer-internet-with-cloudflare/#account-takeover-detection\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;starting in 2024&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, Cloudflare began enabling customers to scan authentication requests for their websites and applications using a &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/privacy-preserving-compromised-credential-checking/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;privacy-preserving&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; compromised credential checker implementation to detect known-leaked usernames and passwords. Today, we&amp;#39;re using aggregated data to display trends in how often these leaked and stolen credentials are observed across Cloudflare&amp;#39;s network. (Here, we are defining âleaked credentialsâ as usernames or passwords being found in a public dataset, or the username and password detected as being similar.)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/waf/detections/leaked-credentials/#how-it-works\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Leaked credentials detection&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; scans incoming HTTP requests for known authentication patterns from common web apps and any custom detection locations that were configured. The service uses a privacy-preserving compromised credential checking protocol to compare a hash of the detected passwords to hashes of compromised passwords found in databases of leaked credentials. A &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer#leaked-credentials-usage\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;new Radar graph&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; on the worldwide &lt;b&gt;Application Layer&lt;/b&gt; Security page provides visibility into aggregate trends around the detection of leaked credentials in authentication requests. Filterable by authentication requests from human users, bots, or all (human + bot), the graph shows the distribution requests classified as âcleanâ (no leaked credentials detected) and âcompromisedâ (leaked credentials, as defined above, were used). At a worldwide level, we found that for the first week of March 2025, leaked credentials were used in 64% of all, over 65% of bot, and over 44% of human authorization requests. &lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/5r8sHbOTrQ1ceGpLa0d3Dn/4ea9ab5b342cd394bd096349d4907ab0/image6.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1834\&quot; height=\&quot;762\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;This suggests that from a human perspective, password reuse is still a problem, as is users not taking immediate actions to change passwords when notified of a breach. And from a bot perspective, this suggests that attackers know that there is a good chance that leaked credentials for one website or application will enable them to access that same userâs account elsewhere.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As a complement to the leaked credentials data, Radar is also now providing a worldwide view into the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer#bot-vs-human\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;share of authentication requests originating from bots&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. Note that not all of these requests are necessarily malicious â while some may be associated with credential stuffing-style attacks, others may be from automated scripts or other benign applications accessing an authentication endpoint. (Having said that, automated malicious attack request volume far exceeds legitimate automated login attempts.) During the first week of March 2025, we found that over 94% of authentication requests came from bots (were automated), with the balance coming from humans. Over that same period, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/traffic?dateStart=2025-03-01&amp;dateEnd=2025-03-07#bot-vs-human\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;bot traffic only accounted for 30% of overall requests&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. So although bots donât represent a majority of request traffic, authentication requests appear to comprise a significant portion of their activity.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/20tYE9sH7SOnJDsnh28ZYz/3c33320a0a7406a348c4b92d22f469ed/image4.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1834\&quot; height=\&quot;762\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;bots-get-a-dedicated-page\&quot;&gt;Bots get a dedicated page&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#bots-get-a-dedicated-page\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;As a reminder, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/en-gb/learning/bots/what-is-a-bot/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;bot&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; traffic describes any non-human Internet traffic, and monitoring bot levels can help spot potential malicious activities. Of course, bots can be helpful too, and Cloudflare maintains a list of &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/bots#verified-bots\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;verified bots&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; to help keep the Internet healthy. Given the importance of monitoring bot activity, we have launched a new dedicated &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/bots\&quot;&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;u&gt;Bots&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/a&gt; page in the Traffic section of Cloudflare Radar to support these efforts. For both worldwide and location views over the selected time period, the page shows the distribution of bot (automated) vs. human HTTP requests, as well as a graph showing bot traffic trends. (Our &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/bots/concepts/bot-score/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;bot score&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, combining machine learning, heuristics, and other techniques, is used to identify automated requests likely to be coming from bots.)Â &lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/5bvoSNcGRbc2I5RGhZoIdo/948eca1e82148a1db6130295c0ea2f42/image2.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1999\&quot; height=\&quot;690\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;Both the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/year-in-review/2023/#bot-traffic-sources\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;2023&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/year-in-review/2024#bot-traffic-sources\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;2024&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; Cloudflare Radar Year in Review microsites included a âBot Traffic Sourcesâ section, showing the locations and networks that Cloudflare determined that the largest shares of &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/bots/concepts/bot-score/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;automated/likely automated&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; traffic was originating from. However, these traffic shares were published just once a year, aggregating traffic from January through the end of November.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In order to provide a more timely perspective, these insights are now available on the new Radar Bots page. Similar to the new DDoS attacks content discussed above, the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/bots#bot-traffic-sources\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;worldwide view&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; includes a choropleth map and table illustrating the locations originating the largest shares of all bot traffic. (Note that a similar &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/traffic#traffic-characteristics\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Traffic Characteristics&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; map and table on the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/traffic\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Traffic Overview page&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; ranks locations by the bot traffic share of the locationâs total traffic.) Similar to Year in Review data linked above, the United States continues to originate the largest share of bot traffic.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/RPCiFgeihzYIbm4XIdQ1r/ae9afa59586266c785e4e9dfa8cb3428/image11.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1834\&quot; height=\&quot;1214\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;In addition, the worldwide view also breaks out &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/bots#bot-traffic-share-by-autonomous-system\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;bot traffic share by AS&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, mirroring the treemap shown in the Year in Review. As we have noted &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/radar-2024-year-in-review/#the-united-states-was-responsible-for-over-a-third-of-global-bot-traffic-amazon-web-services-was-responsible-for-12-7-of-global-bot-traffic-and-7-8-came-from-google\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;previously&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, cloud platform providers account for a significant amount of bot traffic.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/5CsXZNKOZ2ssiVaUzVVM51/5d8ad8b6a257eb0413be19fa101806c9/image8.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1834\&quot; height=\&quot;1018\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;At a location level, depending on the country/region selected, the top sources of bot traffic may be cloud/hosting providers, consumer/business network providers, or a mix. For instance, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/bots/fr#bot-traffic-sources\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Franceâs distribution&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; is shown below, and four ASNs account for just over half of the countryâs bot traffic. Of these ASNs, two (&lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/as16276\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;AS16276&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/as12876\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;AS12876&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;) belong to cloud/hosting providers, and two (&lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/as3215\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;AS3215&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/as12322\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;AS12322&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;) belong to network providers.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/4RM9zvPRa8NzfdTOFMvMcB/b04d53dd725d1890918668775b551004/image9.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1834\&quot; height=\&quot;1018\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;In addition, the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/bots#verified-bots\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Verified Bots list&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; has been moved to the new Bots page on Radar. The data shown and functionality remains unchanged, and links to the old location will automatically be redirected to the new one.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;summary\&quot;&gt;Summary&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#summary\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;The Cloudflare dashboard provides customers with specific views of security trends, application and network layer attacks, and bot activity across their sites and applications. While these views are useful at an individual customer level, aggregated views at a worldwide, location, and network level provide a macro-level perspective on trends and activity. These aggregated views available on Cloudflare Radar not only help customers understand how their observations compare to the larger whole, but they also help the industry understand emerging threats that may require action.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The underlying data for the graphs and data discussed above is available via the Radar API (&lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api/resources/radar/subresources/attacks/subresources/layer7/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Application Layer&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api/resources/radar/subresources/attacks/subresources/layer3/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Network Layer&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api/resources/radar/subresources/http/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Bots&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api/resources/radar/subresources/leaked_credential_checks/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Leaked Credentials&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;). The data can also be interactively explored in more detail across locations, networks, and time periods using Radarâs &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/explorer\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Data Explorer and AI Assistant&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. During 2024âs Security Week, we &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/email-security-insights-on-cloudflare-radar/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;launched an Email Security page&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, which provides a unique perspective on the threats posed by malicious emails, spam volume, the adoption of &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/learning/email-security/dmarc-dkim-spf/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;email authentication methods like SPF, DMARC, and DKIM&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, and the use of IPv4/IPv6 and TLS by email servers. For Security Week 2025, we are adding several new DDoS-focused graphs, new insights into leaked credential trends, and a new &lt;b&gt;Bots&lt;/b&gt; page to Cloudflare Radar.Â  We are also taking this opportunity to refactor Radarâs &lt;b&gt;Security &amp;amp; Attacks&lt;/b&gt; page, breaking it out into &lt;b&gt;Application Layer&lt;/b&gt; and &lt;b&gt;Network Layer&lt;/b&gt; sections.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Below, we review all of these changes and additions to Radar.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;layered-security\&quot;&gt;Layered security&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#layered-security\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;Since Cloudflare Radar launched in 2020, it has included both network layer (Layers 3 &amp;amp; 4) and application layer (Layer 7) attack traffic insights on a single &lt;b&gt;Security &amp;amp; Attacks&lt;/b&gt; page. Over the last four-plus years, we have evolved some of the existing data sets on the page, as well as adding new ones. As the page has grown and improved over time, it risked becoming unwieldy to navigate, making it hard to find the graphs and data of interest. To help address that, the &lt;b&gt;Security&lt;/b&gt; section on Radar now features separate &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer\&quot;&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;u&gt;Application Layer&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/network-layer\&quot;&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;u&gt;Network Layer&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/a&gt; pages. The &lt;b&gt;Application Layer&lt;/b&gt; page is the default, and includes insights from analysis of HTTP-based malicious and attack traffic. The &lt;b&gt;Network Layer&lt;/b&gt; page includes insights from analysis of network and transport layer attacks, as well as observed &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/tcp-resets-timeouts/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;TCP resets and timeouts&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. Future security and attack-related data sets will be added to the relevant page. &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/email-security\&quot;&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;u&gt;Email Security&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/a&gt; remains on its own dedicated page.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/3zxFA8iG7N9MZQzAQAaCNf/ee92891b74b0d70052cc43239dad656f/image__2_.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;2400\&quot; height=\&quot;704\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;a-geographic-and-network-view-of-application-layer-ddos-attacks\&quot;&gt;A geographic and network view of application layer DDoS attacks&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#a-geographic-and-network-view-of-application-layer-ddos-attacks\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;Radarâs &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/reports\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;quarterly DDoS threat reports&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; have historically provided insights, aggregated on a quarterly basis, into the top source and target locations of application layer DDoS attacks. A &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer#application-layer-ddos-attacks-distribution\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;new map and table&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; on Radarâs &lt;b&gt;Application Layer&lt;/b&gt; Security page now provide more timely insights, with a global choropleth map showing a geographical distribution of source and target locations, and an accompanying list of the top 20 locations by share of all DDoS requests. Source location attribution continues to rely on the geolocation of the IP address originating the blocked request, while target location remains the billing location of the account that owns the site being attacked.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Over the first week of March 2025, the United States, Indonesia, and Germany were the top sources of application layer DDoS attacks, together accounting for over 30% of such attacks as shown below. The concentration across the top targeted locations was quite different, with customers from Canada, the United States, and Singapore attracting 56% of application layer DDoS attacks.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/7nW8CyC5L4s68ntQEe1pfT/a8b571b9b2325f5f71936367e8879af1/image10.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1834\&quot; height=\&quot;1216\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;In addition to extended visibility into the geographic source of application layer DDoS attacks, we have also added &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/learning/network-layer/what-is-an-autonomous-system/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;autonomous system (AS)&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;-level visibility. A &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer#application-layer-ddos-attacks-source-as-distribution\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;new treemap&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; view shows the distribution of these attacks by source AS. At a global level, the largest sources include cloud/hosting providers in Germany, the United States, China, and Vietnam.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/332A6OGGKUNCE0wLVBKBmd/2b93c6bd46105602214d7ced2ead71b6/image7.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1834\&quot; height=\&quot;1020\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;For a selected country/region, the treemap displays a source AS distribution for attacks observed to be originating from that location. In some, the sources of attack traffic are heavily concentrated in consumer/business network providers, such as in &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer/pt#application-layer-ddos-attacks-source-as-distribution\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Portugal&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, shown below. However, in other countries/regions that have a large cloud provider presence, such as &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer/ie#application-layer-ddos-attacks-source-as-distribution\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Ireland&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer/sg#application-layer-ddos-attacks-source-as-distribution\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Singapore&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, and the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer/us#application-layer-ddos-attacks-source-as-distribution\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;United States&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, ASNs associated with these types of providers are the dominant sources. To that end, Singapore was listed as being among the top sources of application layer DDoS attacks in each of the quarterly DDoS threat reports in 2024.Â &lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/3RpwYjFjmTR2WFx5UpsUuS/5211c6e5afd4733185ba1ed03750d1d2/image3.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1834\&quot; height=\&quot;1020\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;have-you-been-pwned\&quot;&gt;Have you been pwned?&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#have-you-been-pwned\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;Every week, it seems like thereâs another headline about a data breach, talking about thousands or millions of usernames and passwords being stolen. Or maybe you get an email from an identity monitoring service that your username and password were found on the âdark webâ. (Of course, youâre getting those alerts thanks to a complementary subscription to the service offered as penance from another data breachâ¦)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This credential theft is especially problematic because people often reuse passwords, despite best practices advising the use of strong, unique passwords for each site or application. To help mitigate this risk, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/a-safer-internet-with-cloudflare/#account-takeover-detection\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;starting in 2024&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, Cloudflare began enabling customers to scan authentication requests for their websites and applications using a &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/privacy-preserving-compromised-credential-checking/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;privacy-preserving&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; compromised credential checker implementation to detect known-leaked usernames and passwords. Today, we&amp;#39;re using aggregated data to display trends in how often these leaked and stolen credentials are observed across Cloudflare&amp;#39;s network. (Here, we are defining âleaked credentialsâ as usernames or passwords being found in a public dataset, or the username and password detected as being similar.)&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/waf/detections/leaked-credentials/#how-it-works\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Leaked credentials detection&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; scans incoming HTTP requests for known authentication patterns from common web apps and any custom detection locations that were configured. The service uses a privacy-preserving compromised credential checking protocol to compare a hash of the detected passwords to hashes of compromised passwords found in databases of leaked credentials. A &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer#leaked-credentials-usage\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;new Radar graph&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; on the worldwide &lt;b&gt;Application Layer&lt;/b&gt; Security page provides visibility into aggregate trends around the detection of leaked credentials in authentication requests. Filterable by authentication requests from human users, bots, or all (human + bot), the graph shows the distribution requests classified as âcleanâ (no leaked credentials detected) and âcompromisedâ (leaked credentials, as defined above, were used). At a worldwide level, we found that for the first week of March 2025, leaked credentials were used in 64% of all, over 65% of bot, and over 44% of human authorization requests. &lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/5r8sHbOTrQ1ceGpLa0d3Dn/4ea9ab5b342cd394bd096349d4907ab0/image6.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1834\&quot; height=\&quot;762\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;This suggests that from a human perspective, password reuse is still a problem, as is users not taking immediate actions to change passwords when notified of a breach. And from a bot perspective, this suggests that attackers know that there is a good chance that leaked credentials for one website or application will enable them to access that same userâs account elsewhere.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As a complement to the leaked credentials data, Radar is also now providing a worldwide view into the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/security/application-layer#bot-vs-human\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;share of authentication requests originating from bots&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. Note that not all of these requests are necessarily malicious â while some may be associated with credential stuffing-style attacks, others may be from automated scripts or other benign applications accessing an authentication endpoint. (Having said that, automated malicious attack request volume far exceeds legitimate automated login attempts.) During the first week of March 2025, we found that over 94% of authentication requests came from bots (were automated), with the balance coming from humans. Over that same period, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/traffic?dateStart=2025-03-01&amp;dateEnd=2025-03-07#bot-vs-human\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;bot traffic only accounted for 30% of overall requests&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. So although bots donât represent a majority of request traffic, authentication requests appear to comprise a significant portion of their activity.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/20tYE9sH7SOnJDsnh28ZYz/3c33320a0a7406a348c4b92d22f469ed/image4.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1834\&quot; height=\&quot;762\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;bots-get-a-dedicated-page\&quot;&gt;Bots get a dedicated page&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#bots-get-a-dedicated-page\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;As a reminder, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/en-gb/learning/bots/what-is-a-bot/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;bot&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; traffic describes any non-human Internet traffic, and monitoring bot levels can help spot potential malicious activities. Of course, bots can be helpful too, and Cloudflare maintains a list of &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/bots#verified-bots\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;verified bots&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; to help keep the Internet healthy. Given the importance of monitoring bot activity, we have launched a new dedicated &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/bots\&quot;&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;u&gt;Bots&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/a&gt; page in the Traffic section of Cloudflare Radar to support these efforts. For both worldwide and location views over the selected time period, the page shows the distribution of bot (automated) vs. human HTTP requests, as well as a graph showing bot traffic trends. (Our &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/bots/concepts/bot-score/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;bot score&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, combining machine learning, heuristics, and other techniques, is used to identify automated requests likely to be coming from bots.)Â &lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/5bvoSNcGRbc2I5RGhZoIdo/948eca1e82148a1db6130295c0ea2f42/image2.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1999\&quot; height=\&quot;690\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;Both the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/year-in-review/2023/#bot-traffic-sources\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;2023&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/year-in-review/2024#bot-traffic-sources\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;2024&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; Cloudflare Radar Year in Review microsites included a âBot Traffic Sourcesâ section, showing the locations and networks that Cloudflare determined that the largest shares of &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/bots/concepts/bot-score/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;automated/likely automated&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; traffic was originating from. However, these traffic shares were published just once a year, aggregating traffic from January through the end of November.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In order to provide a more timely perspective, these insights are now available on the new Radar Bots page. Similar to the new DDoS attacks content discussed above, the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/bots#bot-traffic-sources\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;worldwide view&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; includes a choropleth map and table illustrating the locations originating the largest shares of all bot traffic. (Note that a similar &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/traffic#traffic-characteristics\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Traffic Characteristics&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; map and table on the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/traffic\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Traffic Overview page&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; ranks locations by the bot traffic share of the locationâs total traffic.) Similar to Year in Review data linked above, the United States continues to originate the largest share of bot traffic.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/RPCiFgeihzYIbm4XIdQ1r/ae9afa59586266c785e4e9dfa8cb3428/image11.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1834\&quot; height=\&quot;1214\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;In addition, the worldwide view also breaks out &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/bots#bot-traffic-share-by-autonomous-system\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;bot traffic share by AS&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, mirroring the treemap shown in the Year in Review. As we have noted &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/radar-2024-year-in-review/#the-united-states-was-responsible-for-over-a-third-of-global-bot-traffic-amazon-web-services-was-responsible-for-12-7-of-global-bot-traffic-and-7-8-came-from-google\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;previously&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, cloud platform providers account for a significant amount of bot traffic.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/5CsXZNKOZ2ssiVaUzVVM51/5d8ad8b6a257eb0413be19fa101806c9/image8.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1834\&quot; height=\&quot;1018\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;At a location level, depending on the country/region selected, the top sources of bot traffic may be cloud/hosting providers, consumer/business network providers, or a mix. For instance, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/bots/fr#bot-traffic-sources\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Franceâs distribution&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; is shown below, and four ASNs account for just over half of the countryâs bot traffic. Of these ASNs, two (&lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/as16276\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;AS16276&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/as12876\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;AS12876&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;) belong to cloud/hosting providers, and two (&lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/as3215\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;AS3215&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/as12322\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;AS12322&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;) belong to network providers.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/4RM9zvPRa8NzfdTOFMvMcB/b04d53dd725d1890918668775b551004/image9.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1834\&quot; height=\&quot;1018\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;In addition, the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/bots#verified-bots\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Verified Bots list&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; has been moved to the new Bots page on Radar. The data shown and functionality remains unchanged, and links to the old location will automatically be redirected to the new one.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;summary\&quot;&gt;Summary&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#summary\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;The Cloudflare dashboard provides customers with specific views of security trends, application and network layer attacks, and bot activity across their sites and applications. While these views are useful at an individual customer level, aggregated views at a worldwide, location, and network level provide a macro-level perspective on trends and activity. These aggregated views available on Cloudflare Radar not only help customers understand how their observations compare to the larger whole, but they also help the industry understand emerging threats that may require action.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The underlying data for the graphs and data discussed above is available via the Radar API (&lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api/resources/radar/subresources/attacks/subresources/layer7/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Application Layer&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api/resources/radar/subresources/attacks/subresources/layer3/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Network Layer&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api/resources/radar/subresources/http/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Bots&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api/resources/radar/subresources/leaked_credential_checks/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Leaked Credentials&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;). The data can also be interactively explored in more detail across locations, networks, and time periods using Radarâs &lt;a href=\&quot;https://radar.cloudflare.com/explorer\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Data Explorer and AI Assistant&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. This tool empowers your cybersecurity defense with actionable intelligence to stay ahead of attacks and protect your critical assets.&quot;],&quot;featured&quot;:[0,true],&quot;html&quot;:[0,&quot;&lt;p&gt;Today, one of the greatest challenges that cyber defenders face is analyzing detection hits from indicator feeds, which provide metadata about specific indicators of compromise (IOCs), like IP addresses, &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/learning/network-layer/what-is-an-autonomous-system/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;ASNs&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, domains, URLs, and hashes. While indicator feeds have proliferated across the threat intelligence industry, most feeds contain no contextual information about why an indicator was placed on the feed. Another limitation of most feeds today is that they focus solely on blockable indicators and cannot easily accommodate more complex cases, such as a threat actor exploiting a CVE or an insider threat. Instead, this sort of complex threat intelligence is left for long form reporting. However, long-form reporting comes with its own challenges, such as the time required for writing and editing, which can lead to significant delays in releasing timely threat intelligence.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To help address these challenges, we are excited to launch our &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/security-center/cloudforce-one/#analyze-threat-events\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;threat events platform&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; for Cloudforce One customers. Every day, Cloudflare blocks billions of cyber threats. This new platform contains contextual data about the threats we monitor and mitigate on the Cloudflare network and is designed to empower security practitioners and decision makers with actionable insights from a global perspective.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;On average, we process 71 million HTTP requests per second and 44 million DNS queries per second. This volume of traffic provides us with valuable insights and a comprehensive view of current (real-time) threats. The new threat events platform leverages the insights from this traffic to offer a comprehensive, real-time view of threat activity occurring on the Internet, enabling &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/application-services/products/cloudforceone/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Cloudforce One&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; customers to better protect their assets and respond to emerging threats.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;how-we-built-the-threat-events-platform-leveraging-cloudflares-traffic-insights\&quot;&gt;How we built the threat events platform leveraging Cloudflareâs traffic insights&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#how-we-built-the-threat-events-platform-leveraging-cloudflares-traffic-insights\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;The sheer volume of threat activity observed across Cloudflareâs network would overwhelm any system or SOC analyst. So instead, we curate this activity into a stream of events that include not only indicators of compromise (IOCs) but also context, making it easier to take action based on Cloudflareâs unique data. To start off, we expose events related to &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/en-gb/ddos/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;denial of service&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; (DOS) attacks observed across our network, along with the advanced threat operations tracked by our &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/en-gb/application-services/products/cloudforceone/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Cloudforce One Intelligence team&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, like the various tools, techniques, and procedures used by the threat actors we are tracking. We mapped the events to the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://attack.mitre.org/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;MITRE ATT&amp;amp;CK&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; framework and to the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyber_kill_chain\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;cyber kill chain&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; stages. In the future, we will add events related to traffic blocked by our &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/application-services/products/waf/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Web Application Firewall&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; (WAF), Zero Trust &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/zero-trust/products/gateway/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Gateway&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, Zero Trust &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/zero-trust/products/email-security/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Email Security&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; Business Email Compromise, and many other Cloudflare-proprietary datasets. Together, these events will provide our customers with a detailed view of threat activity occurring across the Internet.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Each event in our threat events summarizes specific threat activity we have observed, similar to a &lt;a href=\&quot;https://oasis-open.github.io/cti-documentation/stix/walkthrough#-sighting-object\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;STIX2 sighting object&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and provides contextual information in its summary, detailed view and via the mapping to the MITRE ATT&amp;amp;Ck and KillChain stages. For an example entry, please see the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api/resources/cloudforce_one/subresources/threat_events/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;API documentation&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/7GIiqbsRaSXG6G9RIeHPdQ/fe42f8c5134208b97fc2f16f33c4ee9a/image2.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;Cloudflareâs traffic is used to detect threat events occurring across the Internet. Each event contains valuable contextual information that is provided in the threat events platform.\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1999\&quot; height=\&quot;738\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;Our goal is to empower customers to better understand the threat landscape by providing key information that allows them to investigate and address both broad and specific questions about threats targeting their organization. For example:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Who is targeting my industry vertical?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Who is targeting my country?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;What indicators can I use to block attacks targeting my verticals?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;What has an adversary done across the kill chain over some period of time?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;p&gt;Each event has a unique identifier that links it to the identified threat activity, enabling our Cloudforce One threat intelligence analysts to provide additional context in follow-on investigations.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;how-we-built-the-threat-events-platform-using-cloudflare-workers\&quot;&gt;How we built the threat events platform using Cloudflare Workers&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#how-we-built-the-threat-events-platform-using-cloudflare-workers\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;We chose to use the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/developer-platform/products/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Cloudflare Developer Platform&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; to build out the threat events platform, as it allowed us to leverage the versatility and seamless integration of Cloudflare Workers. At its core, the platform is a Cloudflare Worker that uses &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/sqlite-in-durable-objects/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;SQLite-backed Durable Objects&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; to store events observed on the Cloudflare network. We opted to use Durable Objects over &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/d1/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;D1&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, Cloudflareâs serverless SQL database solution, because it permits us to dynamically create SQL tables to store uniquely customizable datasets. Storing datasets this way allows threat events to scale across our network, so we are resilient to surges in data that might correlate with the unpredictable nature of attacks on the Internet. It also permits us to control events by data source, share a subset of datasets with trusted partners, or restrict access to only authorized users.Â  Lastly, the metadata for each individual threat event is stored in the Durable Object KV so that we may store contextual data beyond our fixed, searchable fields. This data may be in the form of requests-per-second for our denial of service events, or sourcing information so Cloudforce One analysts can tie the event to the exact threat activity for further investigation.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/P2fP5IBVHYCcytcT3xI3m/f0833512bb5d12b41f350847c368e92a/image5.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;Our Cloudforce One threat events architecture is backed by Durable Objects storage for each of our datasets\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1512\&quot; height=\&quot;433\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;how-to-use-threat-events\&quot;&gt;How to use threat events&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#how-to-use-threat-events\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;Cloudforce One customers can access threat events through the Cloudflare Dashboard in &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/security-center/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Security Center&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; or via the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api/resources/cloudforce_one/subresources/threat-events/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Cloudforce One threat events API&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;. Each exposes the stream of threat activity occurring across the Internet as seen by Cloudflare, and are customizable by user-defined filters.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the Cloudflare Dashboard, users have access to an Attacker Timelapse view, designed to answer strategic questions, as well as a more granular events table for drilling down into attack details. This approach ensures that users have the most relevant information at their fingertips.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;h4&gt;Events Table&lt;/h4&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/2QiogugKXB8rGKMZ4cYaSK/506b56c7788648456f085e9fd04d975b/image1.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;Threat events table\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1600\&quot; height=\&quot;1210\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/security-center/cloudforce-one/#analyze-threat-events\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;The events table&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; is a detailed view in the Security Center where users can drill down into specific threat activity filtered by various criteria. It is here that users can explore specific threat events and adversary campaigns using Cloudflareâs traffic insights. Most importantly, this table will provide our users with actionable Indicators of Compromise and an event summary so that they can properly defend their services. All of the data available in our events table is equally accessible via the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api/resources/cloudforce_one/subresources/threat-events/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Cloudforce One threat events API&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To showcase the power of threat events, letâs explore a real-world case:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Recently leaked chats of the &lt;a href=\&quot;https://therecord.media/black-basta-ransomware-group-chat-logs-leaked\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Black Basta&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; criminal enterprise exposed details about their victims, methods, and infrastructure purchases. Although we canât confirm whether the leaked chats were manipulated in any way, the infrastructure discussed in the chats was simple to verify. As a result, this threat intelligence is now available as events in the threat events, along with additional unique Cloudflare context.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Analysts searching for domains, hosts, and file samples used by Black Basta can leverage the threat events to gain valuable insight into this threat actorâs operations. For example, in the threat events UI, a user can filter the âAttackerâ column by selecting âBlackBastaâ in the dropdown, as shown in the image below. This provides a curated list of verified IP addresses, domains, and file hashes for further investigation. For more detailed information on Cloudflareâs unique visibility into Black Basta threat activity see &lt;a href=\&quot;https://www.cloudflare.com/threat-intelligence/research/report/black-bastas-blunder-exploiting-the-gangs-leaked-chats/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;Black Bastaâs blunder: exploiting the gangâs leaked chats&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;figure class=\&quot;kg-card kg-image-card\&quot;&gt;\n          &lt;Image src=\&quot;https://cf-assets.www.cloudflare.com/zkvhlag99gkb/3z9ZtL3n2Ssu1iqPxDF8Fg/4283854ab4cc643b6acb71eb837c944d/image4.png\&quot; alt=\&quot;Example events associated with the Black Basta threat actor group\&quot; class=\&quot;kg-image\&quot; width=\&quot;1642\&quot; height=\&quot;1002\&quot; loading=\&quot;lazy\&quot;/&gt;\n          &lt;/figure&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;why-we-are-publishing-threat-events\&quot;&gt;Why we are publishing threat events&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#why-we-are-publishing-threat-events\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;Our customers face a myriad of cyber threats that can disrupt operations and compromise sensitive data. As adversaries become increasingly sophisticated, the need for timely and relevant threat intelligence has never been more critical. This is why we are introducing threat events, which provides deeper insights into these threats.Â &lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The threat events platform aims to fill this gap by offering a more detailed and contextualized view of ongoing threat activity. This feature allows analysts to self-serve and explore incidents through customizable filters, enabling them to identify patterns and respond effectively. By providing access to real-time threat data, we empower organizations to make informed decisions about their security strategies.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To validate the value of our threat events platform, we had a Fortune 20 threat intelligence team put it to the test. They conducted an analysis against 110 other sources, and we ranked as their #1 threat intelligence source. They found us &amp;quot;very much a unicorn&amp;quot; in the threat intelligence space. Itâs early days, but the initial feedback confirms that our intelligence is not only unique but also delivering exceptional value to defenders.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;whats-next\&quot;&gt;Whatâs next&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#whats-next\&quot; aria-hidden=\&quot;true\&quot; class=\&quot;relative sm:absolute sm:-left-5\&quot;&gt;\n              &lt;svg width=\&quot;16\&quot; height=\&quot;16\&quot; viewBox=\&quot;0 0 24 24\&quot;&gt;&lt;path fill=\&quot;currentcolor\&quot; d=\&quot;m12.11 15.39-3.88 3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1-3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0-3.5l3.88-3.88a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-3.88 3.89a4.48 4.48 0 0 0 6.33 6.33l3.89-3.88a1 1 0 1 0-1.42-1.42Zm8.58-12.08a4.49 4.49 0 0 0-6.33 0l-3.89 3.88a1 1 0 0 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.88a2.52 2.52 0 0 1 3.5 0 2.47 2.47 0 0 1 0 3.5l-3.88 3.88a1 1 0 1 0 1.42 1.42l3.88-3.89a4.49 4.49 0 0 0 0-6.33ZM8.83 15.17a1 1 0 0 0 1.1.22 1 1 0 0 0 .32-.22l4.92-4.92a1 1 0 0 0-1.42-1.42l-4.92 4.92a1 1 0 0 0 0 1.42Z\&quot;&gt;&lt;/path&gt;&lt;/svg&gt;\n            &lt;/a&gt;\n          &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;p&gt;While Cloudforce One customers now have access to our &lt;a href=\&quot;https://developers.cloudflare.com/api/resources/cloudforce_one/subresources/threat-events/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;API&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\&quot;https://dash.cloudflare.com/\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;dashboard&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;, allowing for seamless integration of threat intelligence into their existing systems, they will also soon have access to more visualisations and analytics for the threat events in order to better understand and report back on their findings. This upcoming UI will include enhanced visualizations of attacker timelines, campaign overviews, and attack graphs, providing even deeper insights into the threats facing your organization. Moreover, weâll add the ability to integrate with existing SIEM platforms and share indicators across systems.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Read more about the &lt;a This diverse infrastructure offers flexibility and scalability, but also opens up new attack surfaces.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To support both business continuity and security needs, âsecurity must evolve from being &lt;a href=\&quot;https://blog.cloudflare.com/welcome-to-security-week-2025/#how-can-we-help-make-the-internet-better\&quot;&gt;&lt;u&gt;reactive to predictive&lt;/u&gt;&lt;/a&gt;â. Maintaining a healthy security posture entails monitoring and strengthening your security defenses to identify risks, ensure compliance, and protect against evolving threats. With our newest capabilities, you can now use Cloudflare to achieve a healthy posture across your SaaS and web applications. This addresses any security teamâs ultimate (daily) question: &lt;i&gt;How well are our assets and documents protected&lt;/i&gt;?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A predictive security posture relies on the following key components:&lt;/p&gt;&lt;ul&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Real-time discovery and inventory of all your assets and documents&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Continuous asset-aware threat detection and risk assessment&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;li&gt;&lt;p&gt;Prioritised remediation suggestions to increase your protection&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/li&gt;&lt;/ul&gt;&lt;p&gt;Today, we are sharing how we have built these key components across SaaS and web applications, and how you can use them to manage your businessâs security posture.&lt;/p&gt;\n          &lt;div class=\&quot;flex anchor relative\&quot;&gt;\n            &lt;h3 id=\&quot;your-security-posture-at-a-glance\&quot;&gt;Your security posture at a glance&lt;/h3&gt;\n            &lt;a href=\&quot;#your-security-posture-at-a-glance\&quot;
